<p><b>Franklin Pierce</b> (November 23, 1804&#160;– October 8, 1869) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">14th President of the United States</a> (1853–1857). Pierce was a northern <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a> who saw the <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolitionist movement</a> as a fundamental threat to the unity of the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> His polarizing actions in championing and signing the <a href="/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska_Act" title="Kansas–Nebraska Act">Kansas–Nebraska Act</a> and enforcing the <a href="/wiki/Fugitive_Slave_Act" title="Fugitive Slave Act" class="mw-redirect">Fugitive Slave Act</a> failed to stem intersectional conflict, setting the stage for Southern secession.</p>
<p>Born in <a href="/wiki/History_of_New_Hampshire" title="History of New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>, Pierce served in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._House_of_Representatives" title="U.S. House of Representatives" class="mw-redirect">U.S. House of Representatives</a> and the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Senate" title="U.S. Senate" class="mw-redirect">Senate</a> until he resigned from the latter in 1842. His private <a href="/wiki/Lawyer" title="Lawyer">law practice</a> in his home state was a success; he was appointed <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_District_of_New_Hampshire#U.S._Attorney_for_New_Hampshire" title="United States District Court for the District of New Hampshire">U.S. Attorney</a> for his state in 1845. Pierce took part in the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_general_(United_States)" title="Brigadier general (United States)">brigadier general in the Army</a>. Seen by Democrats as a compromise candidate uniting northern and southern interests, he was nominated as the party's candidate for president on the 49th ballot at the <a href="/wiki/1852_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1852 Democratic National Convention">1852 Democratic National Convention</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1852" title="U.S. presidential election, 1852" class="mw-redirect">1852 presidential election</a>, Pierce and his running mate <a href="/wiki/William_R._King" title="William R. King">William R. King</a> easily defeated the <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a> ticket of <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Alexander_Graham" title="William Alexander Graham">William A. Graham</a>.</p>
<p>While Pierce was popular and outgoing, his family life was a grim affair, with his wife <a href="/wiki/Jane_Pierce" title="Jane Pierce">Jane</a> suffering from illness and depression for much of her life.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> All of their children died young, their last son being gruesomely killed in a train accident while the family was traveling shortly before Pierce's inauguration. As president, Pierce simultaneously attempted to enforce neutral standards for civil service while also satisfying the diverse elements of the Democratic Party with patronage, an effort which largely failed and turned many in his party against him. Pierce was a <a href="/wiki/Young_America_movement" title="Young America movement">Young America</a> expansionist who signed the <a href="/wiki/Gadsden_Purchase" title="Gadsden Purchase">Gadsden Purchase</a> of land from Mexico and led a failed attempt to acquire Cuba from Spain. He signed trade treaties with Britain and Japan, while his Cabinet reformed their departments and improved accountability, but these successes were overshadowed by political strife.</p>
<p>His popularity in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Northern_states" title="U.S. Northern states" class="mw-redirect">Northern states</a> declined sharply after he supported the Kansas–Nebraska Act, which nullified the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a>, while many whites in the South continued to support him. Passage of the act led to <a href="/wiki/Bleeding_Kansas" title="Bleeding Kansas">violent conflict</a> over the expansion of slavery in the <a href="/wiki/American_West" title="American West" class="mw-redirect">American West</a>. Pierce's administration was further damaged when several of his diplomats issued the <a href="/wiki/Ostend_Manifesto" title="Ostend Manifesto">Ostend Manifesto</a>, calling for the annexation of Cuba, a document which was roundly criticized. Although Pierce fully expected to be renominated by the Democrats in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1856" title="U.S. presidential election, 1856" class="mw-redirect">1856 presidential election</a>, he was abandoned by his party and his bid failed. His reputation in the North suffered further during the Civil War as he became a vocal critic of President <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>. Pierce, who had been a heavy drinker for much of his life, died of severe <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis_of_the_liver" title="Cirrhosis of the liver" class="mw-redirect">cirrhosis of the liver</a> in 1869.</p>
<p>US historians and other political commentators generally <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States#Notable_scholar_surveys" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">rank Pierce's presidency among the worst</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family">Early life and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pierce_Birthplace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of a white house." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Pierce_Birthplace.jpg/220px-Pierce_Birthplace.jpg" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Pierce_Birthplace.jpg/330px-Pierce_Birthplace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Pierce_Birthplace.jpg/440px-Pierce_Birthplace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="545" data-file-height="488" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pierce_Birthplace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce_Homestead" title="Franklin Pierce Homestead">Franklin Pierce Homestead</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hillsborough,_New_Hampshire" title="Hillsborough, New Hampshire">Hillsborough</a>, where Pierce grew up, is now a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-homestead_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-homestead-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> He was born in a nearby <a href="/wiki/Log_cabin" title="Log cabin">log cabin</a> as the homestead was being completed.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>note 1<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_and_education">Childhood and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Childhood and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Franklin Pierce was born on November 23, 1804, in a <a href="/wiki/Log_cabin" title="Log cabin">log cabin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hillsborough,_New_Hampshire" title="Hillsborough, New Hampshire">Hillsborough</a>, in the northeastern state of <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>. He was a sixth-generation descendant of Thomas Pierce, who had moved to the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bay_Colony" title="Massachusetts Bay Colony">Massachusetts Bay Colony</a> from <a href="/wiki/Norwich,_Norfolk" title="Norwich, Norfolk" class="mw-redirect">Norwich, County Norfolk</a>, England, in about 1634. Pierce's father <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Pierce_(governor)" title="Benjamin Pierce (governor)">Benjamin</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_War" title="Revolutionary War" class="mw-redirect">Revolutionary War</a> lieutenant, moved from <a href="/wiki/Chelmsford,_Massachusetts" title="Chelmsford, Massachusetts">Chelmsford, Massachusetts</a> to Hillsborough after the war, purchasing 50 acres (20&#160;ha) of land. Pierce was the fifth of eight children born to Benjamin and his second wife, Anna Kendrick (his first wife Elizabeth Andrews died in childbirth, leaving a daughter). Benjamin was by then a prominent <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican</a><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>note 2<span>]</span></a></sup> state legislator, farmer, and tavern-keeper. During Pierce's childhood his father was deeply involved in state politics, while two of his older brothers fought in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>; public affairs and the military were thus a major influence in his early life.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce's father, who sought to ensure that his sons were educated, placed Pierce in a school at Hillsborough Center in childhood and sent him to the town school at Hancock at the age of 12.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> The boy was not fond of schooling. Growing homesick at Hancock, he walked 12 miles (19&#160;km) back to his home one Sunday. His father fed him dinner and drove him part of the distance back to school before kicking him out of the carriage and ordering him to walk the rest of the way in a thunderstorm. Pierce later cited this moment as "the turning-point in my life".<sup id="cite_ref-turning_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-turning-10"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Later that year he transferred to <a href="/wiki/Phillips_Exeter_Academy" title="Phillips Exeter Academy">Phillips Exeter Academy</a> to prepare for college. By this time he had built a reputation as a charming student, sometimes prone to misbehavior.<sup id="cite_ref-turning_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-turning-10"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nathaniel_Hawthorne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Portrait of Nathaniel Hawthorne" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Nathaniel_Hawthorne.jpg/220px-Nathaniel_Hawthorne.jpg" width="220" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Nathaniel_Hawthorne.jpg/330px-Nathaniel_Hawthorne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Nathaniel_Hawthorne.jpg/440px-Nathaniel_Hawthorne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="574" data-file-height="700" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nathaniel_Hawthorne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Novelist <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Hawthorne" title="Nathaniel Hawthorne">Nathaniel Hawthorne</a> remained lifelong friends with Pierce. He wrote a glowing biography, <i>The Life of Franklin Pierce</i>, in support of Pierce's 1852 presidential campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In fall 1820, Pierce entered <a href="/wiki/Bowdoin_College" title="Bowdoin College">Bowdoin College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brunswick,_Maine" title="Brunswick, Maine">Brunswick, Maine</a>, one of nineteen freshmen. He joined the Athenian Society, a progressive literary society, alongside <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Cilley" title="Jonathan Cilley">Jonathan Cilley</a> (later elected to Congress) and <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Hawthorne" title="Nathaniel Hawthorne">Nathaniel Hawthorne</a> (the author), with whom he formed lasting friendships. Last in his class after two years, he worked hard to improve his grades and in 1824 graduated in fifth place<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> in a graduating class of fourteen.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> In Pierce's junior year, <a href="/wiki/John_P._Hale" title="John P. Hale">John P. Hale</a>, a later political ally and then rival of Pierce's, also enrolled at Bowdoin. That year, Pierce organized and led an unofficial militia company, the Bowdoin Cadets, which included Cilley and Hawthorne. The unit performed drill on campus near the president's house until the noise caused him to demand it halt. The students rebelled and went on strike, an event Pierce was suspected of leading.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> During his final year at Bowdoin, he spent several months teaching at a school in rural <a href="/wiki/Hebron,_Maine" title="Hebron, Maine">Hebron, Maine</a>, where his students included future Congressman <a href="/wiki/John_J._Perry" title="John J. Perry">John J. Perry</a>, and he earned his first salary.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After briefly <a href="/wiki/Read_law" title="Read law" class="mw-redirect">reading law</a> with former New Hampshire Governor <a href="/wiki/Levi_Woodbury" title="Levi Woodbury">Levi Woodbury</a>, a family friend, in <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth,_New_Hampshire" title="Portsmouth, New Hampshire">Portsmouth</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> he spent a semester at <a href="/wiki/Northampton_Law_School" title="Northampton Law School">Northampton Law School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northampton,_Massachusetts" title="Northampton, Massachusetts">Northampton, Massachusetts</a>, followed by a period of study in 1826 and 1827 under Judge Edmund Parker in <a href="/wiki/Amherst,_New_Hampshire" title="Amherst, New Hampshire">Amherst, New Hampshire</a>. He was admitted to the bar in late 1827 and began to practice in Hillsborough.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce lost his first case, but soon proved capable as a lawyer. Despite never being a legal scholar, his memory for names and faces served him well, as did his personal charm and deep voice.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> In Hillsborough, his law partner was Albert Baker, who had studied law under Pierce and was the brother of <a href="/wiki/Mary_Baker_Eddy" title="Mary Baker Eddy">Mary Baker Eddy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_politics">State politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: State politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By 1824, New Hampshire was a hotbed of partisanship, with figures such as Woodbury and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Hill" title="Isaac Hill">Isaac Hill</a> laying the groundwork for a party of Democrats in support of General <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>. They opposed the established <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalists</a> (and their successors, the <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Party" title="National Republican Party">National Republicans</a>), who were led by sitting President <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>. The work of the New Hampshire Democratic Party came to fruition in March 1827, when their pro-Jackson nominee, Benjamin Pierce, won the support of the pro-Adams faction and was elected governor of New Hampshire essentially unopposed. While the younger Pierce had set out to build a career as an attorney, he was fully drawn into the realm of politics as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">1828 presidential election</a> between Adams and Jackson approached. In the state elections held in March 1828, the Adams faction withdrew their support of Benjamin Pierce, voting him out of office,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>note 3<span>]</span></a></sup> but Franklin Pierce won his first election: Hillsborough <a href="/wiki/Town_meeting#Traditional_town_meetings" title="Town meeting">town moderator</a>, a position to which he would be elected for six consecutive years.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce actively campaigned in his district on behalf of Jackson, who carried both the district and the nation by large margins in the November 1828 election, even though he <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_in_New_Hampshire,_1828" title="United States presidential election in New Hampshire, 1828">lost New Hampshire</a>. The outcome further strengthened the Democratic Party, and Pierce won his first legislative seat the following year, representing Hillsborough in the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_House_of_Representatives" title="New Hampshire House of Representatives">New Hampshire House of Representatives</a>. Pierce's father, meanwhile, was elected again as governor, retiring after that term. The younger Pierce was appointed as chairman of the House Education Committee in 1829 and the Committee on Towns the following year. By 1831 the Democrats held a legislative majority, and Pierce was elected Speaker of the House. The young Speaker used his platform to oppose the expansion of banking, protect the state militia, and offer support to the national Democrats and Jackson's re-election effort. At the age of 27, he was a star of the New Hampshire Democratic Party. Though attaining early political and professional success, in his personal letters he continued to lament his bachelorhood and yearned for a life beyond Hillsborough.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Like all white males in New Hampshire between the ages of 18 and 45, Pierce was a member of the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Army_National_Guard" title="New Hampshire Army National Guard">state militia</a>, and was appointed <a href="/wiki/Aide_de_camp" title="Aide de camp" class="mw-redirect">aide de camp</a> to Governor <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Dinsmoor" title="Samuel Dinsmoor">Samuel Dinsmoor</a> in 1831. He remained in the militia until 1847, and attained the rank of <a href="/wiki/Colonel_(United_States)" title="Colonel (United States)">colonel</a> before becoming a <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_General_(United_States)" title="Brigadier General (United States)" class="mw-redirect">brigadier general</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">Army</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> Interested in revitalizing and reforming the state militias, which had become increasingly dormant during the years of peace following the War of 1812, Pierce worked with <a href="/wiki/Alden_Partridge" title="Alden Partridge">Alden Partridge</a>, president of <a href="/wiki/Norwich_University" title="Norwich University">Norwich University</a>, a military college in <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Truman_B._Ransom" title="Truman B. Ransom">Truman B. Ransom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alonzo_Jackman" title="Alonzo Jackman">Alonzo Jackman</a>, Norwich faculty members and militia officers, to increase recruiting efforts and improve training and readiness.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce served as a Norwich University trustee from 1841 to 1859, and received the <a href="/wiki/Honorary_degree" title="Honorary degree">honorary degree</a> of <a href="/wiki/Legum_Doctor" title="Legum Doctor">LL.D.</a> from Norwich in 1853.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In late 1832, the Democratic Party convention nominated Pierce for one of New Hampshire's five seats in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</a>. This was <a href="/wiki/Tantamount_to_election" title="Tantamount to election">tantamount to election</a> for the young Democrat, as the National Republicans had faded as a political force, while the <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whigs</a> had not yet begun to attract a large following. Democratic strength in New Hampshire was also bolstered by <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1832" title="United States presidential election, 1832">Jackson's landslide re-election that year</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-w44plus_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w44plus-29"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> New Hampshire had been a marginal state politically, but from 1832 through the mid-1850s became the most reliably Democratic state in the North, boosting Pierce's political career.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce's term began in March 1833, but he would not be sworn in until Congress met in December, and his attention was elsewhere. He had recently become engaged and bought his first house in Hillsborough. Franklin and Benjamin Pierce were among the prominent citizens who welcomed President Jackson to the state on his visit in mid-1833.<sup id="cite_ref-w44plus_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w44plus-29"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_children">Marriage and children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Marriage and children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jane_Appleton_Pierce.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Engraving of Jane Pierce" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Jane_Appleton_Pierce.JPG/220px-Jane_Appleton_Pierce.JPG" width="220" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Jane_Appleton_Pierce.JPG/330px-Jane_Appleton_Pierce.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Jane_Appleton_Pierce.JPG/440px-Jane_Appleton_Pierce.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1805" data-file-height="2153" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jane_Appleton_Pierce.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pious and reserved, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Pierce" title="Jane Pierce">Jane Pierce</a> was her husband's opposite in many ways.<sup id="cite_ref-jane_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jane-31"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On November 19, 1834, Pierce married <a href="/wiki/Jane_Pierce" title="Jane Pierce">Jane Means Appleton</a> (March 12, 1806&#160;– December 2, 1863), the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Appleton" title="Jesse Appleton">Jesse Appleton</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregational</a> minister and former president of Bowdoin College, and Elizabeth Means. The Appletons were prominent Whigs, in contrast with the Pierces' Democratic affiliation. Jane was shy, devoutly religious, and pro-<a href="/wiki/Temperance_movement" title="Temperance movement">temperance</a>, encouraging Pierce to abstain from alcohol. She was somewhat gaunt, and constantly ill from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> and psychological ailments. She abhorred politics and especially disliked Washington, D.C., creating a tension that would continue throughout Pierce's political ascent.<sup id="cite_ref-jane_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jane-31"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jane disliked Hillsborough as well, and in 1838, the Pierces relocated to the state capital, <a href="/wiki/Concord,_New_Hampshire" title="Concord, New Hampshire">Concord, New Hampshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> They had three sons, all of whom died in childhood. Franklin, Jr. (February 2–5, 1836) died in infancy, while Frank Robert (August 27, 1839 – November 14, 1843) died at the age of four from <a href="/wiki/Epidemic_typhus" title="Epidemic typhus">epidemic typhus</a>. Benjamin (April 13, 1841 – January 6, 1853) <a href="/wiki/Andover,_Massachusetts#Death_of_President-elect_Franklin_Pierce.27s_son" title="Andover, Massachusetts">died at the age of 11</a> <a href="#Tragedy_and_transition">in a train accident</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Congressional_career">Congressional career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Congressional career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._House_of_Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: U.S. House of Representatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pierce departed in November 1833 for Washington, D.C., where the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-third_United_States_Congress" title="Twenty-third United States Congress" class="mw-redirect">Twenty-third United States Congress</a> convened its regular session on December 2. Jackson's second term was under way, and the House had a strong Democratic majority, whose primary focus was to prevent the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">Second Bank of the United States</a> from being rechartered. The Democrats, including Pierce, defeated proposals supported by the newly formed Whig Party, and the bank's charter expired. Pierce broke from his party on occasion, opposing Democratic bills to fund <a href="/wiki/Internal_improvements" title="Internal improvements">internal improvements</a> with federal money. He saw both the bank and infrastructure spending as unconstitutional, with internal improvements the responsibility of the states. Pierce's first term was fairly uneventful from a legislative standpoint, and he was easily re-elected in March 1835. When not in Washington, he attended to his law practice, and in December 1835 returned to the capital for the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-fourth_United_States_Congress" title="Twenty-fourth United States Congress" class="mw-redirect">Twenty-fourth Congress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolitionism</a> grew more vocal in the mid-1830s, Congress was inundated with petitions from anti-slavery groups seeking legislative action to restrict slavery in the United States. From the beginning, Pierce found the abolitionists' "agitation" to be an annoyance, and saw federal action against slavery as an infringement on southern states' rights, even though he was morally opposed to slavery itself.<sup id="cite_ref-wallner-i-57-59_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wallner-i-57-59-35"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> He was also frustrated with the "religious bigotry" of abolitionists, who cast their political opponents as sinners.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> "I consider slavery a social and political evil," Pierce said, "and most sincerely wish that it had no existence upon the face of the earth."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Still, he wrote in December 1835, "One thing must be perfectly apparent to every intelligent man. This abolition movement must be crushed or there is an end to the Union."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When Rep. <a href="/wiki/James_Henry_Hammond" title="James Henry Hammond">James Henry Hammond</a> of South Carolina looked to prevent anti-slavery petitions from reaching the House floor, however, Pierce sided with the abolitionists' right to petition. Nevertheless, Pierce supported what came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/21st_Rule" title="21st Rule">gag rule</a>, which allowed for petitions to be received, but not read or considered. This passed the House in 1836.<sup id="cite_ref-wallner-i-57-59_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wallner-i-57-59-35"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> He was attacked by the New Hampshire anti-slavery <i>Herald of Freedom</i> as a "<a href="/wiki/Doughface" title="Doughface">doughface</a>", which had the dual meaning of "craven-spirited man" and "northerner with southern sympathies".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce had stated that not one in five hundred New Hampshirites were abolitionists; the article added up the number of signatures on petitions from that state and divided by the census figure. He was outraged when South Carolina Senator <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a> read the article on the Senate floor. After Pierce gave a speech indicating that most signatories were women and children, without the vote, Calhoun apologized.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Senate">U.S. Senate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: U.S. Senate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Calhoun_by_Longacre.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Portrait of John C. Calhoun" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Calhoun_by_Longacre.jpg/220px-Calhoun_by_Longacre.jpg" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/Calhoun_by_Longacre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/Calhoun_by_Longacre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="310" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Calhoun_by_Longacre.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Senator <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a> was one of the major powers in the Senate during Pierce's term there. Engraving by <a href="/wiki/James_B._Longacre" title="James B. Longacre">James B. Longacre</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The resignation in May 1836 of Senator Isaac Hill, who had been elected governor of New Hampshire, left a brief interim opening to be filled by the state legislature. With Hill's term as senator due to expire in March 1837, the legislature also had to fill the six-year term to follow. Pierce's candidacy for the Senate was championed by state Representative John P. Hale, a fellow Athenian at Bowdoin. After much debate, the legislature chose <a href="/wiki/John_Page_(New_Hampshire)" title="John Page (New Hampshire)">John Page</a> to fill the rest of Hill's term. In December 1836, Pierce was elected to the full term, to commence in March 1837, becoming at age 32 one of the youngest member in Senate history to that point. The election came at a difficult time for Pierce, as his father, sister, and brother were all seriously ill, while Jane continued to suffer from chronic poor health. As senator, he was able to help his old friend Nathaniel Hawthorne, who often struggled financially, procuring a <a href="/wiki/Sinecure" title="Sinecure">sinecure</a> as measurer of coal and salt at the Boston Customs House that allowed the author time to continue writing.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce voted the party line on most issues. He was an able senator, but not an eminent one; he was overshadowed by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Triumvirate" title="Great Triumvirate">Great Triumvirate</a> of Calhoun, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a>, who dominated the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce entered the Senate at a time of economic crisis, as the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a> had begun. He considered the depression a result of the banking system's rapid growth, amidst "the extravagance of overtrading and the wilderness of speculation". So that federal money would not support speculative bank loans, he supported newly elected Democratic president <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a> and his plan to create an <a href="/wiki/Independent_Treasury" title="Independent Treasury">independent treasury</a>, a proposal which split the Democratic Party. Debate over slavery continued in Congress, and abolitionists proposed its end in the District of Columbia, where Congress had jurisdiction. Pierce supported a resolution by Calhoun against this proposal, which Pierce considered a dangerous stepping stone to nationwide emancipation.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Meanwhile, the Whigs were growing in congressional strength, which would leave Pierce's party with only a small majority by the end of the decade.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>One topic of particular importance to Pierce was the military. He challenged a bill which would expand the ranks of the Army's staff officers in Washington without any apparent benefit to line officers at posts in the rest of the country. He took an interest in military pensions, seeing abundant fraud within the system, and was named chairman of the Senate Committee on Military Pensions in the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-sixth_United_States_Congress" title="Twenty-sixth United States Congress" class="mw-redirect">Twenty-sixth Congress</a> (1839–1841). In that capacity, he urged the modernization and expansion of the Army, with a focus on militias and mobility rather than on coastal fortifications, which he considered outdated.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce campaigned vigorously throughout his home state for Van Buren's re-election in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1840" title="United States presidential election, 1840">1840 presidential election</a>. The incumbent carried New Hampshire but lost the national vote to <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a>, the military hero, whose Whigs took a majority of seats in the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-seventh_United_States_Congress" title="Twenty-seventh United States Congress" class="mw-redirect">Twenty-seventh Congress</a>. Harrison died after a month in office, and Vice President <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a> succeeded him. Pierce and the Democrats were quick to challenge the new administration, questioning the removal of federal officeholders, and opposing Whig plans for a national bank. In December 1841 Pierce decided to resign from Congress, something he had been planning for some time.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> New Hampshire Democrats felt that no one should hold one of the state's Senate seats for longer than one six-year term. Thus, he had little likelihood of re-election. Also, he was frustrated at being a member of the legislative minority and wished to devote his time to his family and law practice.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> His last acts in the Senate before resigning in February 1842 were to oppose a bill distributing federal funds to the states – believing that the money should go to the military instead – and to challenge the Whigs to reveal the results of their investigation of the <a href="/wiki/Collector_of_the_Port_of_New_York" title="Collector of the Port of New York">New York Customs House</a>, where the Whigs had probed for Democratic corruption for nearly a year but had issued no findings.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Party_leader">Party leader</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Party leader">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawyer_and_politician">Lawyer and politician</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Lawyer and politician">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Piercemanse.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of a white house" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Piercemanse.JPG/220px-Piercemanse.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Piercemanse.JPG/330px-Piercemanse.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Piercemanse.JPG/440px-Piercemanse.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Piercemanse.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Concord house where Pierce lived from 1842 to 1848 is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Pierce_Manse" title="Pierce Manse">Pierce Manse</a>. The house was restored in the 1970s and is now maintained as a historic attraction.<sup id="cite_ref-manse_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-manse-49"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite his resignation from the Senate, Pierce had no intention of leaving public life. The move to Concord had given him more opportunities for cases, and allowed Jane a more robust community life.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Jane had remained in Concord with her young son Frank and her newborn Benjamin for the latter part of Pierce's Senate term, and this separation had taken a toll on the family. Pierce, meanwhile, had begun a demanding but lucrative law partnership with <a href="/wiki/Asa_Fowler" title="Asa Fowler">Asa Fowler</a> during congressional recesses.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce returned to Concord in early 1842, and his reputation as a lawyer continued to flourish. Known for his gracious personality, eloquence, and excellent memory, Pierce would attract large audiences to hear him in court. He would often represent poor people for little or no compensation.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce remained involved in the state Democratic Party, which was split by several issues. Governor Hill, who represented the commercial, urban wing of the party, advocated the use of government charters to support corporations, granting them privileges such as limited liability and eminent domain for building railroads. The radical "<a href="/wiki/Locofocos" title="Locofocos">locofoco</a>" wing of his party represented farmers and other rural voters, who sought an expansion of social programs and labor regulations and a restriction on corporate privilege. The state's political culture grew less tolerant of banks and corporations in the wake of the Panic of 1837, and Hill was voted out of office. Pierce was closer to the radicals philosophically, and reluctantly served as attorney against Hill in a dispute regarding ownership of a newspaper—Hill lost, and founded his own, in which Pierce was a frequent target.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In June 1842 Pierce was named chairman of the State Democratic Committee. In the following year's state election he helped the radical wing take over the state legislature. The party remained divided on several issues, including railroad development and the temperance movement, and Pierce took a leading role in helping the state legislature settle their differences. His priorities were "order, moderation, compromise, and party unity", which he tried to place ahead of his personal views on political issues.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> As he would as president, Pierce valued Democratic Party unity highly, and saw opposition to slavery as a threat to that.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The surprise victory of <a href="/wiki/Dark_horse" title="Dark horse">dark horse</a> Democratic candidate <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1844" title="United States presidential election, 1844">1844 presidential election</a> was welcome news to Pierce, who had befriended the former <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker of the House</a> while both served in Congress. Pierce had campaigned heavily for Polk during the election, and in turn Polk appointed him <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_District_of_New_Hampshire#U.S._Attorney_for_New_Hampshire" title="United States District Court for the District of New Hampshire">United States Attorney for New Hampshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Polk's most prominent cause was the <a href="/wiki/Annexation_of_Texas" title="Annexation of Texas" class="mw-redirect">annexation of Texas</a>, an issue which caused a dramatic split between Pierce and his former ally Hale, now a U.S. Representative. Hale was so impassioned against adding a new slave state that he wrote a letter to 1,400 Democrats in New Hampshire outlining his opposition to the measure. Pierce responded by re-assembling the state Democratic convention to revoke Hale's nomination for another term in Congress. The political firestorm led to Pierce cutting off ties with his longtime friend, and with his law partner Fowler, who was a Hale supporter.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Hale refused to withdraw, and as a majority vote was needed for election in New Hampshire, the party split led to deadlock and a vacant House seat. Eventually, the Whigs and Hale's <a href="/wiki/Independent_Democrat" title="Independent Democrat">Independent Democrats</a> took control of the legislature, elected Whig <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Colby" title="Anthony Colby">Anthony Colby</a> as governor and sent Hale to the Senate, much to Pierce's anger.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexican.E2.80.93American_War">Mexican–American War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mexican–American War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin_Pierce_-_1852.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Franklin_Pierce_-_1852.jpg/170px-Franklin_Pierce_-_1852.jpg" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Franklin_Pierce_-_1852.jpg/255px-Franklin_Pierce_-_1852.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Franklin_Pierce_-_1852.jpg/340px-Franklin_Pierce_-_1852.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="760" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin_Pierce_-_1852.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pierce in his Brigadier General's uniform.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Active military service was a long-held dream for Pierce, who had admired his father's and brothers' service in his youth, particularly his older brother <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Kendrick_Pierce" title="Benjamin Kendrick Pierce">Benjamin</a>'s, as well as that of <a href="/wiki/John_McNeil_Jr." title="John McNeil Jr.">John McNeil Jr.</a>, husband of Pierce's older half-sister Elizabeth. As a legislator, he was a passionate advocate for volunteer militias. As a militia officer himself, he had experience mustering and drilling bodies of troops. When Congress declared war against Mexico in May 1846, Pierce immediately volunteered to join, although no New England regiment yet existed. His hope to fight in the war was one reason he refused an offer to become Polk's <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">Attorney General</a>. General <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a>'s advance slowed in northern Mexico, and General <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a> proposed capturing the port of <a href="/wiki/Veracruz,_Veracruz" title="Veracruz, Veracruz" class="mw-redirect">Vera Cruz</a> and driving overland to Mexico City. Congress passed a bill authorizing the creation of ten regiments, and Pierce was appointed colonel and commander of the <a href="/wiki/9th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="9th Infantry Regiment (United States)">9th Infantry Regiment</a> in February 1847, with Truman B. Ransom as <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Colonel_(United_States)" title="Lieutenant Colonel (United States)" class="mw-redirect">lieutenant colonel</a> and second-in-command.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FrankP-mounted.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Illustration of Franklin Pierce as a general, riding a horse" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/FrankP-mounted.jpg/220px-FrankP-mounted.jpg" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/FrankP-mounted.jpg/330px-FrankP-mounted.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/FrankP-mounted.jpg/440px-FrankP-mounted.jpg 2x" data-file-width="810" data-file-height="1067" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FrankP-mounted.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pierce's brief term as a <a href="/wiki/General_officer" title="General officer">general</a> in the Mexican–American War boosted his public image.<sup id="cite_ref-war-reception_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-war-reception-60"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On March 3, 1847, Pierce was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_general_(United_States)" title="Brigadier general (United States)">brigadier general</a>, and took command of a <a href="/wiki/Brigade" title="Brigade">brigade</a> of reinforcements for General Scott's army, with Ransom succeeding to command of the regiment. Needing time to assemble his brigade, Pierce reached the already-seized port of Vera Cruz in late June, where he prepared a march of 2,500 men accompanying supplies to take to Scott. The three-week journey inland was perilous, and the men fought off several attacks before joining with Scott's army in early August, in time for the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Contreras" title="Battle of Contreras">Battle of Contreras</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> The battle was disastrous for Pierce: his horse was suddenly startled during a charge, knocking him groin-first against his saddle. The horse then tripped into a crevice and fell, pinning Pierce underneath and leaving him with a debilitating knee injury.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> The incident made it look like he had fainted, causing one soldier to call for someone else to take command, "General Pierce is a damned coward."<sup id="cite_ref-h505_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h505-63"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce returned for the following day's action, but again injured his knee, forcing him to hobble after his men, and by the time he caught up, the battle had mostly been won.<sup id="cite_ref-h505_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h505-63"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Churubusco" title="Battle of Churubusco">Battle of Churubusco</a> approached, Pierce was ordered by Scott to the rear. He responded, "For God's sake, General, this is the last great battle, and I must lead my brigade." Scott yielded, and Pierce entered the fight tied to his saddle, but the pain in his leg became so great that he passed out on the field. The Americans won the battle and Pierce helped negotiate an armistice. He then returned to command and led his brigade throughout the rest of the campaign, eventually taking part in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_for_Mexico_City" title="Battle for Mexico City">capture of Mexico City</a> in mid-September, although his brigade was held in reserve for much of the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-w147plus_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w147plus-64"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> He spent much of that battle in the sick tent, plagued with acute diarrhea.<sup id="cite_ref-h505_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-h505-63"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce remained in command of his brigade during the three-month occupation of the city, while frustrated with the stalling of peace negotiations, and tried to distance himself from the constant conflict between Scott and the other generals.<sup id="cite_ref-w147plus_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w147plus-64"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce was finally allowed to return to Concord in late December 1847. He was given a hero's welcome in his home state and issued his resignation from the Army, which was approved on March 20, 1848. His military exploits elevated his popularity in New Hampshire, but his injuries and subsequent troubles in battle led to accusations of cowardice which would long follow him. He had demonstrated competence as a general, especially in the initial march from Vera Cruz, but his short tenure and his injury left little for historians to judge his ability as a military commander.<sup id="cite_ref-war-reception_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-war-reception-60"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, who had the opportunity to observe Pierce firsthand during the war, countered the allegations of cowardice in his memoirs, written several years after Pierce's death: "Whatever General Pierce's qualifications may have been for the Presidency, he was a gentleman and a man of courage. I was not a supporter of him politically, but I knew him more intimately than I did any other of the volunteer generals."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_New_Hampshire">Return to New Hampshire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Return to New Hampshire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frank-Pierce.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Daguerreotype of Franklin Pierce" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Frank-Pierce.jpg/220px-Frank-Pierce.jpg" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Frank-Pierce.jpg/330px-Frank-Pierce.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Frank-Pierce.jpg/440px-Frank-Pierce.jpg 2x" data-file-width="638" data-file-height="812" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frank-Pierce.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
By the 1850s, Pierce had become a <i>de facto</i> leader of the New Hampshire Democratic Party.<sup id="cite_ref-leadership_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leadership-66"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Returning to Concord, Pierce resumed his law work; in one notable case he defended the religious liberty of the <a href="/wiki/Shakers" title="Shakers">Shakers</a>, the insular sect who were being threatened with legal action over accusations of abuse. His role as a party leader, however, continued to take up most of his attention. He continued to wrangle with Senator Hale, who was stridently anti-slavery and had opposed the war, stances that Pierce regarded as needless agitation.<sup id="cite_ref-leadership_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leadership-66"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The large <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Cession" title="Mexican Cession">Mexican Cession</a> of land had divided the United States politically, with many in the North insisting that slavery not be allowed there (and offering the <a href="/wiki/Wilmot_Proviso" title="Wilmot Proviso">Wilmot Proviso</a> to ensure it), while others wanted slavery barred north of the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a> line of <a href="/wiki/Parallel_36%C2%B030%E2%80%B2_north" title="Parallel 36°30? north">36°30? N</a>. Both proposals were anathema to Southerners, and the controversy divided the Democrats. At the <a href="/wiki/1848_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1848 Democratic National Convention">1848 Democratic National Convention</a>, the majority nominated former Michigan senator <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Cass" title="Lewis Cass">Lewis Cass</a> for president, while a minority broke off to become the <a href="/wiki/Free_Soil_Party" title="Free Soil Party">Free Soil Party</a>, backing former president Van Buren. The Whigs chose General Taylor, a Louisianan, whose views on most political issues were unknown. Despite his past support for Van Buren, Pierce supported Cass, turning down the quiet offer of second place on the Free Soil <a href="/wiki/Ticket_(politics)" title="Ticket (politics)" class="mw-redirect">ticket</a>, and was so effective that Taylor, who was <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1848" title="United States presidential election, 1848">elected president</a>, was held in New Hampshire to his lowest percentage in any state.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Senator Henry Clay, a Whig, hoped to put the slavery question to rest with what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1850" title="Compromise of 1850">Compromise of 1850</a>, which would give some victories to both slaveholders and abolitionists, and gained the support of his fellow Whig, Webster. With the proposal stalled in the Senate, Illinois Senator <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a> led an effort to split it into separate bills so that each legislator could vote against the parts his state opposed without endangering the overall package. This was done, and the bills passed, to be signed by President <a href="/wiki/Millard_Fillmore" title="Millard Fillmore">Millard Fillmore</a> (who had succeeded Taylor after the president's death earlier in 1850).<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce strongly supported the compromise, giving a well-received speech in December 1850 pledging himself to "The Union! Eternal Union!"<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-69"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> The same month, the Democratic candidate for governor, John Atwood, issued a letter opposing the Compromise, and Pierce helped to recall the state convention and remove Atwood from the ticket.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-69"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> The fiasco would compromise the election for the Democrats, who lost several races. Still, Pierce's party retained its control over the state, and was well positioned for the upcoming presidential election.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1852">Election of 1852</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Election of 1852">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/1852_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1852 Democratic National Convention">1852 Democratic National Convention</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1852" title="United States presidential election, 1852">United States presidential election, 1852</a></div>
<p>As the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1852" title="United States presidential election, 1852">1852 presidential election</a> approached, the Democrats were divided by the slavery issue, though most of the <a href="/wiki/Barnburners_and_Hunkers#Barnburners" title="Barnburners and Hunkers">"Barnburners"</a> who had left the party with Van Buren to form the Free Soil Party had returned. It was widely expected that the <a href="/wiki/1852_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1852 Democratic National Convention">1852 Democratic National Convention</a> would result in deadlock, with no major candidate able to win the necessary two-thirds majority. New Hampshire Democrats, including Pierce, supported his old teacher, Levi Woodbury, by then an <a href="/wiki/Associate_Justice_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States">Associate Justice of the Supreme Court</a>, as a compromise candidate, but Woodbury's death in September 1851 opened up an opportunity for Pierce's allies to present him as a potential dark horse in the mold of Polk. New Hampshire Democrats felt that, as the state in which their party had most consistently gained majorities, they should supply the presidential candidate. Other possible standard-bearers included Douglas, Cass, <a href="/wiki/William_Marcy" title="William Marcy" class="mw-redirect">William Marcy</a> of New York, <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a> of Pennsylvania, <a href="/wiki/Sam_Houston" title="Sam Houston">Sam Houston</a> of Texas, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hart_Benton_(politician)" title="Thomas Hart Benton (politician)">Thomas Hart Benton</a> of Missouri.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the backing of his home state, Pierce faced obstacles in gaining the nomination, as he had not held elective office in a decade, and lacked the front-runners' national reputation. He publicly declared that such a nomination would be "utterly repugnant to my tastes and wishes", but given the desire of New Hampshire Democrats to see one of their own elected, knew that his position as a party leader would be endangered if he was unwilling to run.<sup id="cite_ref-w184plus_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w184plus-72"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Thus, he quietly allowed his supporters to lobby for him, with the understanding that his name would not be entered at the convention unless it was clear none of the front-runners could win. To broaden his potential base of southern support as the convention approached, he wrote letters reiterating his support for the Compromise of 1850, including the controversial <a href="/wiki/Fugitive_Slave_Act" title="Fugitive Slave Act" class="mw-redirect">Fugitive Slave Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-w184plus_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w184plus-72"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1852DemocraticCampaignPoster.png" class="image"><img alt="Poster" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/1852DemocraticCampaignPoster.png/220px-1852DemocraticCampaignPoster.png" width="220" height="296" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/1852DemocraticCampaignPoster.png/330px-1852DemocraticCampaignPoster.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/1852DemocraticCampaignPoster.png/440px-1852DemocraticCampaignPoster.png 2x" data-file-width="475" data-file-height="640" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1852DemocraticCampaignPoster.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Campaign poster for the Pierce/King ticket</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The convention assembled on June 1 in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, Maryland, and the deadlock occurred as expected. The first ballot was taken on June 3. Of 288 delegates, Cass claimed 116, Buchanan 93, and the rest were scattered, without a single vote for Pierce. The next 34 ballots passed with no one near victory, and still no votes for Pierce. The Buchanan team decided to have their delegates vote for minor candidates, including Pierce, to demonstrate that no one but Buchanan could win. It was hoped that once delegates realized this, the convention would unite behind Buchanan. This novel tactic backfired after several ballots as Virginia, New Hampshire, and Maine switched to Pierce; the remaining Buchanan forces began to break for Marcy, and before long Pierce was in third place. After the 48th ballot, North Carolina Congressman <a href="/wiki/James_C._Dobbin" title="James C. Dobbin">James C. Dobbin</a> delivered an unexpected and passionate endorsement of Pierce, sparking a wave of support for the dark horse candidate. On the 49th ballot, Pierce received all but six of the votes, and thus gained the Democratic nomination for president. Delegates selected Alabama Senator <a href="/wiki/William_R._King" title="William R. King">William R. King</a>, a Buchanan supporter, as Pierce's <a href="/wiki/Running_mate" title="Running mate">running mate</a>, and adopted a <a href="/wiki/Party_platform" title="Party platform">party platform</a> that rejected further "agitation" over the slavery issue and supported the Compromise of 1850.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When word reached New Hampshire of the result, Pierce found it difficult to believe, and his wife fainted. Their son Benjamin wrote to his mother hoping that Franklin's candidacy would not be successful, as he knew she would not like to live in Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scott_vs_Pierce_campaign.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Political cartoon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Scott_vs_Pierce_campaign.jpg/350px-Scott_vs_Pierce_campaign.jpg" width="350" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Scott_vs_Pierce_campaign.jpg/525px-Scott_vs_Pierce_campaign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Scott_vs_Pierce_campaign.jpg/700px-Scott_vs_Pierce_campaign.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1257" data-file-height="753" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scott_vs_Pierce_campaign.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This anti-Pierce political cartoon depicts him as weak and cowardly</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Whig candidate was General Scott, whom Pierce had served under in Mexico; his running mate was <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="United States Secretary of the Navy">Secretary of the Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Alexander_Graham" title="William Alexander Graham">William A. Graham</a>. The Whigs could not unify their factions as the Democrats had, and the convention adopted a platform almost indistinguishable from that of the Democrats, including support of the Compromise of 1850. This incited the Free Soilers to field their own candidate, Senator Hale of New Hampshire, at the expense of the Whigs. The lack of political differences reduced the campaign to a bitter personality contest and helped to dampen <a href="/wiki/Voter_turnout" title="Voter turnout">voter turnout</a> in the election to its lowest level since <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1836" title="U.S. presidential election, 1836" class="mw-redirect">1836</a>; it was, according to Pierce biographer Peter A. Wallner, "one of the least exciting campaigns in presidential history".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> Scott was harmed by the lack of enthusiasm of anti-slavery northern Whigs for the candidate and platform; <i><a href="/wiki/New-York_Tribune" title="New-York Tribune">New-York Tribune</a></i> editor <a href="/wiki/Horace_Greeley" title="Horace Greeley">Horace Greeley</a> summed up the attitude of many when he said of the Whig platform, "we defy it, execrate it, spit upon it".<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1852.svg" class="image"><img alt="Map" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/ElectoralCollege1852.svg/350px-ElectoralCollege1852.svg.png" width="350" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/ElectoralCollege1852.svg/525px-ElectoralCollege1852.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/ElectoralCollege1852.svg/700px-ElectoralCollege1852.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1852.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Electoral map of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1852" title="United States presidential election, 1852">1852 presidential election</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pierce kept quiet so as not to upset his party's delicate unity, and allowed his allies to run the campaign. It was the custom at the time for candidates to not appear to seek the office, and he did no personal campaigning.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce's opponents caricatured him as a coward and alcoholic ("the hero of many a well-fought bottle"), who was an anti-Catholic.<sup id="cite_ref-caricature_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caricature-78"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> Scott, meanwhile, drew weak support from the Whigs, who were torn by their pro-Compromise platform and found him to be an abysmal, gaffe-prone public speaker.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> The Democrats were confident: a popular slogan was that the Democrats "will <i>pierce</i> their enemies in 1852 as they <i>poked</i> [that is, <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">Polked</a>] them in 1844."<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> This proved to be true, as Scott won only Kentucky, Tennessee, Massachusetts and Vermont, finishing with 42 electoral votes to Pierce's 254. With 3.2 million votes cast, Pierce won the popular vote with 50.9 to 44.1 percent. A sizable block of Free Soilers broke for Pierce's in-state rival, Hale, who won 4.9 percent of the popular vote.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> The Democrats took large majorities in Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency">Presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tragedy_and_transition">Tragedy and transition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tragedy and transition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:First_ladies-pierce.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of Jane and Benjamin Pierce" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/First_ladies-pierce.jpg/220px-First_ladies-pierce.jpg" width="220" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/First_ladies-pierce.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/First_ladies-pierce.jpg 2x" data-file-width="275" data-file-height="352" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:First_ladies-pierce.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jane Pierce and "Benny", whose death cast a shadow over Pierce's term in office.<sup id="cite_ref-benny_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-benny-83"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pierce began his presidency in mourning. Weeks after his election, on January 6, 1853, the President-elect's family had been traveling from Boston by train when their car derailed and rolled down an <a href="/wiki/Embankment_(transportation)" title="Embankment (transportation)">embankment</a> near <a href="/wiki/Andover,_Massachusetts" title="Andover, Massachusetts">Andover, Massachusetts</a>. Pierce and Jane survived, but in the wreckage found their only remaining son, 11-year-old Benjamin, crushed to death, his body nearly decapitated. Pierce was not able to hide the gruesome sight from Jane. They both suffered severe depression afterward, which likely affected Pierce's performance as president.<sup id="cite_ref-benny_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-benny-83"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Jane wondered if the train accident was divine punishment for her husband's pursuit and acceptance of high office. She wrote a lengthy letter of apology to "Benny" for her failings as a mother.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Jane would avoid social functions for much of her first two years as <a href="/wiki/First_Lady_of_the_United_States" title="First Lady of the United States">First Lady</a>, making her public debut in that role to great sympathy at the public reception held at the White House on New Year's Day, 1855.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jane remained in New Hampshire as Pierce departed for his inauguration, which she did not attend. Pierce, the youngest man to be elected president to that point, chose to <a href="/wiki/Affirmation_in_law" title="Affirmation in law">affirm</a> his oath of office on a law book rather than swear it on a Bible, as all his predecessors except John Quincy Adams had done. He was the first president to deliver his <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_Day" title="Inauguration Day" class="mw-redirect">inaugural address</a> from memory. In the address he hailed an era of peace and prosperity at home and urged a vigorous assertion of U.S. interests in its foreign relations, including the "eminently important" acquisition of new territories. "The policy of my Administration", said the new president, "will not be deterred by any timid forebodings of evil from expansion." Avoiding the word "slavery", he emphasized his desire to put the "important subject" to rest and maintain a peaceful union. He alluded to his own personal tragedy, telling the crowd, "You have summoned me in my weakness, you must sustain me by your strength."<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_political_strife">Administration and political strife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Administration and political strife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_Franklin_Pierce" title="List of federal judges appointed by Franklin Pierce">List of federal judges appointed by Franklin Pierce</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -50px; left: -45px; width: 325px"><a href="/wiki/File:PIERCE,_Franklin-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Pierce as president" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/PIERCE%2C_Franklin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/325px-PIERCE%2C_Franklin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="325" height="406" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/PIERCE%2C_Franklin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/488px-PIERCE%2C_Franklin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/PIERCE%2C_Franklin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/650px-PIERCE%2C_Franklin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8328" data-file-height="10405" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PIERCE,_Franklin-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:PIERCE, Franklin-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:PIERCE, Franklin-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Pierce as president</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In his <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States" title="Cabinet of the United States">Cabinet</a> appointments, Pierce sought to unite a party that was squabbling over the fruits of victory. Most of the party had not originally supported him for the nomination, and some had allied with the Free Soil party to gain victory in local elections. Pierce decided to allow each of the party's factions some appointments, even those that had not supported the Compromise of 1850.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>All of Pierce's cabinet nominations were confirmed unanimously and immediately by the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-wall5plus_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wall5plus-88"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce spent the first few weeks of his term sorting through hundreds of lower-level federal positions to be filled. This was a chore, as he sought to represent all factions of the party, and could fully satisfy none of them. Partisans found themselves unable to secure positions for their friends, which put the Democratic Party on edge and fueled bitterness between factions. Before long, northern newspapers accused Pierce of filling his government with pro-slavery secessionists, while southern newspapers accused him of abolitionism.<sup id="cite_ref-wall5plus_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wall5plus-88"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Factionalism between the pro- and anti-administration Democrats ramped up quickly, especially within the New York Democratic Party.
