The more conservative Hardshell Democrats or "Hards" of New York were deeply skeptical of the Pierce administration, which was associated with Marcy (who became Secretary of State) and the more moderate New York faction, the Softshell Democrats or "Softs".<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_R._D._King_Vice_President.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of William R. King" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/William_R._D._King_Vice_President.jpg/170px-William_R._D._King_Vice_President.jpg" width="170" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/William_R._D._King_Vice_President.jpg/255px-William_R._D._King_Vice_President.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/William_R._D._King_Vice_President.jpg/340px-William_R._D._King_Vice_President.jpg 2x" data-file-width="481" data-file-height="549" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_R._D._King_Vice_President.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pierce's Vice President <a href="/wiki/William_R._King" title="William R. King">William R. King</a> died a little more than one month into his term, leaving a vacancy that could not be filled.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Buchanan had urged Pierce to consult Vice President-elect King in selecting the Cabinet, but Pierce did not do so—Pierce and King never communicated once they had been selected as candidates in June 1852. By the start of 1853, King was severely ill with tuberculosis, and went to Cuba to recuperate. His condition deteriorated, and Congress passed a special law, allowing him to be sworn in before the American consul in Havana on March 24. Wanting to die at home, he returned to his plantation in Alabama on April 17 and died the next day. The office of vice president remained vacant for the remainder of Pierce's term, as the Constitution had no provision for filling the vacancy, making <a href="/wiki/President_pro_tempore_of_the_United_States_Senate" title="President pro tempore of the United States Senate">Senate President pro tempore</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Atchison" title="David Atchison" class="mw-redirect">David Atchison</a> of Missouri next in line to the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce sought to run a more efficient and accountable government than his predecessors.<sup id="cite_ref-wallner-20_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wallner-20-91"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> His Cabinet members implemented an early system of <a href="/wiki/United_States_federal_civil_service" title="United States federal civil service">civil service</a> examinations which was a forerunner to the <a href="/wiki/Pendleton_Act" title="Pendleton Act" class="mw-redirect">Pendleton Act</a> passed three decades later.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Interior" title="United States Department of the Interior">Interior Department</a> was reformed by Secretary <a href="/wiki/Robert_McClelland_(American_politician)" title="Robert McClelland (American politician)">Robert McClelland</a>, who systematized its operations, expanded the use of paper records, and pursued fraud.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Another of Pierce's reforms was to expand the role of the U.S. attorney general in appointing federal judges and attorneys, which was an important step in the eventual development of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wallner-20_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wallner-20-91"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> There was a vacancy on the Supreme Court—Fillmore, having failed to get Senate confirmation for his nominees, had offered it to newly elected Louisiana Senator <a href="/wiki/Judah_P._Benjamin" title="Judah P. Benjamin">Judah P. Benjamin</a>, who had declined. Pierce also offered the seat to Benjamin, and when the Louisianan persisted in his refusal,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> nominated instead <a href="/wiki/John_Archibald_Campbell" title="John Archibald Campbell">John Archibald Campbell</a>, an advocate of states' rights; this would be Pierce's only Supreme Court appointment.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_policy_and_internal_improvements">Economic policy and internal improvements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Economic policy and internal improvements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pierce_Indian_Peace_Medal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of Indian Peace Medal" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Pierce_Indian_Peace_Medal.jpg/220px-Pierce_Indian_Peace_Medal.jpg" width="220" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Pierce_Indian_Peace_Medal.jpg/330px-Pierce_Indian_Peace_Medal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Pierce_Indian_Peace_Medal.jpg/440px-Pierce_Indian_Peace_Medal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1539" data-file-height="1668" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pierce_Indian_Peace_Medal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Indian_Peace_Medal" title="Indian Peace Medal">Indian Peace Medal</a> depicting Pierce</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pierce charged <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Treasury Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/James_Guthrie_(Kentucky)" title="James Guthrie (Kentucky)">James Guthrie</a> with reforming the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Treasury" title="United States Treasury" class="mw-redirect">Treasury</a>, which was inefficiently managed, with many unsettled accounts. Guthrie increased oversight of Treasury employees and tariff collectors, many of whom were withholding money from the government. Despite laws requiring funds to be held in the Treasury, large deposits remained in private banks under the Whig administrations. Guthrie reclaimed these funds, and sought to prosecute corrupt officials, with mixed success.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a>, at Pierce's request, led surveys with the <a href="/wiki/Corps_of_Topographical_Engineers" title="Corps of Topographical Engineers">Corps of Topographical Engineers</a> of possible transcontinental railroad routes throughout the country. The Democratic Party had long rejected federal appropriations for internal improvements, but Davis felt that such a project could be justified as a Constitutional national security objective. Davis also deployed the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="United States Army Corps of Engineers">Army Corps of Engineers</a> to supervise construction projects in the District of Columbia, including the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a> and building of the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument" title="Washington Monument">Washington Monument</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_and_military_affairs">Foreign and military affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Foreign and military affairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Pierce administration fell in line with the expansionist <a href="/wiki/Young_America_movement" title="Young America movement">Young America</a> movement, with <a href="/wiki/William_L._Marcy" title="William L. Marcy">William L. Marcy</a> leading the charge as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>. Marcy sought to present to the world a distinctively American, republican image. He issued a circular recommending that U.S. diplomats wear "the simple dress of an American citizen" instead of the elaborate <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_uniform" title="Diplomatic uniform">diplomatic uniforms</a> worn in the courts of Europe, and that they only hire American citizens to work in consulates.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Marcy received international praise for his 73-page letter defending Austrian refugee <a href="/wiki/Martin_Koszta" title="Martin Koszta" class="mw-redirect">Martin Koszta</a>, who had been captured abroad in mid-1853 by the Austrian government despite his intention to become a U.S. citizen.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of Franklin Pierce" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6635" data-file-height="7830" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Franklin Pierce, 1853, by <a href="/wiki/George_Peter_Alexander_Healy" title="George Peter Alexander Healy">George Peter Alexander Healy</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Davis, an advocate of a southern transcontinental route, persuaded Pierce to send rail magnate <a href="/wiki/James_Gadsden" title="James Gadsden">James Gadsden</a> to Mexico to buy land for a potential railroad. Gadsden was also charged with re-negotiating the provisions of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo" title="Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</a> which required the U.S. to prevent Native American raids into Mexico from New Mexico Territory. Gadsden negotiated a treaty with Mexican President <a href="/wiki/Antonio_L%C3%B3pez_de_Santa_Anna" title="Antonio López de Santa Anna">Antonio López de Santa Anna</a> in December 1853, purchasing a large swath of land to America's southwest. Negotiations were nearly derailed by <a href="/wiki/William_Walker_(filibuster)" title="William Walker (filibuster)">William Walker</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Filibuster_(military)" title="Filibuster (military)">unauthorized expedition</a> into Mexico, and so a clause was included charging the U.S. with combating future such attempts.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress reduced the <a href="/wiki/Gadsden_Purchase" title="Gadsden Purchase">Gadsden Purchase</a> to the region now comprising southern <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> and part of southern New Mexico; the price was cut from $15&#160;million to $10&#160;million. Congress also included a protection clause for a private citizen, Albert G. Sloo, whose interests were threatened by the purchase. Pierce opposed the use of the federal government to prop up private industry and did not endorse the final version of the treaty, which was ratified nonetheless.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> The acquisition brought the <a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">contiguous United States</a> to its present-day boundaries, excepting later minor adjustments.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table class="infobox" cellspacing="5" style="width: auto; text-align:left; line-height: 1.2em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 1em; float: left; clear: left;">

<p>Relations with the United Kingdom were tense, as American fishermen felt menaced by the British navy's increasing enforcement of Canadian waters. Marcy completed a <a href="/wiki/Trade_agreement" title="Trade agreement">trade reciprocity agreement</a> with British minister to Washington, <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Crampton,_2nd_Baronet" title="Sir John Crampton, 2nd Baronet">John Crampton</a>, which would reduce the need for aggressive coastline enforcement. Buchanan was sent as minister to London to pressure the British government, which was slow to support a new treaty. A favorable reciprocity treaty was ratified in August 1854, which Pierce saw as a first step towards the American annexation of Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> While the administration negotiated with Britain over the Canadian border, U.S. interests were also threatened in Central America, where the <a href="/wiki/Clayton%E2%80%93Bulwer_Treaty" title="Clayton–Bulwer Treaty">Clayton–Bulwer Treaty</a> of 1850 had failed to keep Great Britain from expanding its influence. Gaining the advantage over Britain in the region was a key part of Pierce's expansionist goals.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>British consuls in the United States sought to enlist Americans for the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> in 1854, in violation of neutrality laws, and Pierce eventually expelled minister Crampton and three consuls. To the President's surprise, the British did not expel Buchanan in retaliation. In his December 1855 <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union" title="State of the Union">message to Congress</a> Pierce had set forth the American case that Britain had violated the Clayton-Bulwer Treaty. The British, according to Buchanan, were impressed by the message and were rethinking their policy. Nevertheless, Buchanan was not successful in getting the British to renounce their Central American possessions. The Canadian treaty was ratified by Congress, the British Parliament, and by the colonial legislatures in Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce's administration aroused sectional apprehensions when three U.S. diplomats in Europe drafted a proposal to the president to purchase <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> from Spain for $120&#160;million (USD), and justify the "wresting" of it from Spain if the offer were refused. The publication of the <a href="/wiki/Ostend_Manifesto" title="Ostend Manifesto">Ostend Manifesto</a>, which had been drawn up at the insistence of Secretary of State Marcy, provoked the scorn of northerners who viewed it as an attempt to annex a slave-holding possession to bolster Southern interests. It helped discredit the expansionist policy of <a href="/wiki/Manifest_Destiny" title="Manifest Destiny" class="mw-redirect">Manifest Destiny</a> the Democratic Party had often supported.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce favored expansion and a substantial reorganization of the military. Secretary of War Davis and Navy Secretary James C. Dobbin found the Army and Navy in poor condition, with insufficient forces, a reluctance to adopt new technology, and inefficient management.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Under the Pierce administration, Commodore <a href="/wiki/Matthew_C._Perry" title="Matthew C. Perry">Matthew C. Perry</a> <a href="/wiki/Perry_Expedition" title="Perry Expedition">visited Japan</a> (a venture originally planned under Fillmore) in an effort to expand trade to the East. Perry wanted to encroach on Asia by force, but Pierce and Dobbin pushed him to remain diplomatic. Perry signed a modest trade treaty with the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">shogunate</a> which was successfully ratified.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> The 1856 launch of the <a href="/wiki/USS_Merrimack_(1855)" title="USS Merrimack (1855)">USS <i>Merrimac</i></a>, one of six newly commissioned <a href="/wiki/Steam_frigate" title="Steam frigate">steam frigates</a>, was one of Pierce's "most personally satisfying" days in office.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bleeding_Kansas">Bleeding Kansas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Bleeding Kansas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska_Act" title="Kansas–Nebraska Act">Kansas–Nebraska Act</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bleeding_Kansas" title="Bleeding Kansas">Bleeding Kansas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1855_Colton_Map_of_Kansas_and_Nebraska_(first_edition)_-_Geographicus_-_NebraskaKansas-colton-1855.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Map" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/1855_Colton_Map_of_Kansas_and_Nebraska_%28first_edition%29_-_Geographicus_-_NebraskaKansas-colton-1855.jpg/350px-1855_Colton_Map_of_Kansas_and_Nebraska_%28first_edition%29_-_Geographicus_-_NebraskaKansas-colton-1855.jpg" width="350" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/1855_Colton_Map_of_Kansas_and_Nebraska_%28first_edition%29_-_Geographicus_-_NebraskaKansas-colton-1855.jpg/525px-1855_Colton_Map_of_Kansas_and_Nebraska_%28first_edition%29_-_Geographicus_-_NebraskaKansas-colton-1855.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/1855_Colton_Map_of_Kansas_and_Nebraska_%28first_edition%29_-_Geographicus_-_NebraskaKansas-colton-1855.jpg/700px-1855_Colton_Map_of_Kansas_and_Nebraska_%28first_edition%29_-_Geographicus_-_NebraskaKansas-colton-1855.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3251" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1855_Colton_Map_of_Kansas_and_Nebraska_(first_edition)_-_Geographicus_-_NebraskaKansas-colton-1855.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska_Act" title="Kansas–Nebraska Act">Kansas–Nebraska Act</a> organized <a href="/wiki/Kansas_Territory" title="Kansas Territory">Kansas</a> (in pink) and <a href="/wiki/Nebraska_Territory" title="Nebraska Territory">Nebraska Territory</a> (yellow).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The greatest challenge to the country's equilibrium during the Pierce administration was the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska_Act" title="Kansas–Nebraska Act">Kansas–Nebraska Act</a>. <a href="/wiki/Organized_territory" title="Organized territory" class="mw-redirect">Organizing</a> the largely unsettled <a href="/wiki/Nebraska_Territory" title="Nebraska Territory">Nebraska Territory</a>, which stretched from <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a>, and from Texas north to what is now the Canadian border, was a crucial part of Douglas's plans for western expansion. He wanted a transcontinental railroad with a link from <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> to California, through the vast western territory. Organizing the territory was necessary for settlement as the land would not be surveyed nor put up for sale until a territorial government was authorized. Those from slave states had never been content with western limits on slavery, and felt it should be able to expand into territories procured with blood and treasure that had come, in part, from the South. Douglas and his allies planned to organize the territory and let local settlers <a href="/wiki/Popular_sovereignty_in_the_United_States" title="Popular sovereignty in the United States">decide whether to allow slavery</a>. This would repeal the Missouri Compromise of 1820, as most of it was north of the 36°30? N line the Missouri Compromise deemed "free". The territory would be split into a northern part, Nebraska, and a southern part, <a href="/wiki/Kansas_Territory" title="Kansas Territory">Kansas</a>, and the expectation was that Kansas would allow slavery and Nebraska would not.<sup id="cite_ref-kansas_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kansas-110"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> In the view of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Southern_states" title="U.S. Southern states" class="mw-redirect">Southern states</a>, the Compromise of 1850 had already annulled the Missouri Compromise by admitting the state of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, including territory south of the compromise line, as a free state.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce had wanted to organize the Nebraska Territory without explicitly addressing the matter of slavery, but Douglas could not get enough southern support to accomplish this.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce was skeptical of the bill, knowing it would result in bitter opposition from the North. Douglas and Davis convinced him to support the bill regardless. It was tenaciously opposed by northerners such as Ohio Senator <a href="/wiki/Salmon_P._Chase" title="Salmon P. Chase">Salmon P. Chase</a> and Massachusetts' <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sumner" title="Charles Sumner">Charles Sumner</a>, who rallied public sentiment in the North against the bill. Northerners had been suspicious of the Gadsden Purchase, moves towards Cuba annexation, and the influence of slaveholding Cabinet members such as Davis, and saw the Nebraska bill as part of a pattern of southern aggression. The result was a political firestorm that did great damage to Pierce's presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-kansas_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kansas-110"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce and his administration used threats and promises to keep most Democrats on board in favor of the bill. The Whigs split along sectional lines; the conflict destroyed them as a national party. The Kansas–Nebraska Act was passed in May 1854 and would come to define the Pierce presidency. The political turmoil that followed the passage saw the short-term influence of the nativist and anti-Catholic American Party, often called the <a href="/wiki/Know_Nothing" title="Know Nothing">Know Nothings</a>, and the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kansas_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kansas-110"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Forcing_Slavery_Freesoilers_Throats.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Political cartoon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Forcing_Slavery_Freesoilers_Throats.jpg/250px-Forcing_Slavery_Freesoilers_Throats.jpg" width="250" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Forcing_Slavery_Freesoilers_Throats.jpg/375px-Forcing_Slavery_Freesoilers_Throats.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Forcing_Slavery_Freesoilers_Throats.jpg/500px-Forcing_Slavery_Freesoilers_Throats.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1467" data-file-height="930" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Forcing_Slavery_Freesoilers_Throats.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Northerners resented Pierce's attempted expansion of slavery through Kansas–Nebraska and Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-resentment_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-resentment-113"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> In this 1856 cartoon, a <a href="/wiki/Free_Soil_Party" title="Free Soil Party">Free Soiler</a> is held down by Pierce, Buchanan, and Cass while Douglas shoves "Slavery" (depicted as a black man) down his throat.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Even as the act was being debated, settlers on both sides of the slavery issue poured into the territories so as to secure the outcome they wanted in the voting. The passage of the act resulted in so much violence between groups that the territory became known as <a href="/wiki/Bleeding_Kansas" title="Bleeding Kansas">Bleeding Kansas</a>. Thousands of pro-slavery <a href="/wiki/Border_Ruffians" title="Border Ruffians" class="mw-redirect">Border Ruffians</a> came across from Missouri to vote in the territorial elections although they were not resident in Kansas, giving that element the victory. Pierce supported the outcome despite the irregularities. When <a href="/wiki/Free-Stater_(Kansas)" title="Free-Stater (Kansas)">Free-Staters</a> set up a shadow government, and drafted the <a href="/wiki/Topeka_Constitution" title="Topeka Constitution">Topeka Constitution</a>, Pierce called their work an act of rebellion. The president continued to recognize the pro-slavery legislature, which was dominated by Democrats, even after a Congressional investigative committee found its election to have been illegitimate. He dispatched federal troops to break up a meeting of the <a href="/wiki/Topeka,_Kansas" title="Topeka, Kansas">Topeka</a> government.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Passage of the act coincided with the seizure of escaped slave <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Burns" title="Anthony Burns">Anthony Burns</a> in Boston. Northerners rallied in support of Burns, but Pierce was determined to follow the Fugitive Slave Act to the letter, and dispatched federal troops to enforce the return to his Virginia owner despite furious crowds.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The midterm congressional elections of 1854 and 1855 were devastating to the Democrats (as well as to the Whig Party, which was on its last legs). The Democrats lost almost every state outside the South. The administration's opponents in the North worked together to return opposition members to Congress, though only a few northern Whigs gained election. In Pierce's New Hampshire, hitherto loyal to the Democratic Party, the Know-Nothings elected the governor, all three representatives, dominated the legislature, and returned Hale to the Senate. Anti-immigrant fervor brought the Know-Nothings their highest numbers to that point, and some northerners were elected under the auspices of the new Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-resentment_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-resentment-113"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1856_election">1856 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 1856 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1856_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1856 Democratic National Convention">1856 Democratic National Convention</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Southern_Chivalry.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Political cartoon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Southern_Chivalry.jpg/300px-Southern_Chivalry.jpg" width="300" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Southern_Chivalry.jpg/450px-Southern_Chivalry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/31/Southern_Chivalry.jpg 2x" data-file-width="565" data-file-height="370" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Southern_Chivalry.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Partisan violence spilled into Congress in May 1856 when Free Soil Senator <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sumner" title="Charles Sumner">Charles Sumner</a> was <a href="/wiki/Caning_of_Charles_Sumner" title="Caning of Charles Sumner">assaulted with a walking cane</a> by Democratic Rep. <a href="/wiki/Preston_Brooks" title="Preston Brooks">Preston Brooks</a> in the Senate chamber.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pierce fully expected to be renominated by the Democrats. In reality his chances of winning the nomination were slim, let alone re-election. The administration was widely disliked in the North for its position on the Kansas–Nebraska Act, and Democratic leaders were aware of Pierce's electoral vulnerability. Nevertheless, his supporters began to plan for an alliance with Stephen A. Douglas to deny James Buchanan the nomination. Buchanan had solid political connections and had been safely overseas through most of Pierce's term, leaving him untainted by the Kansas debacle.<sup id="cite_ref-1856-convention_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1856-convention-117"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When balloting began on June 5 at the convention in <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati,_Ohio" title="Cincinnati, Ohio" class="mw-redirect">Cincinnati, Ohio</a>, Pierce expected a plurality, if not the required two-thirds majority. On the first ballot, he received only 122 votes, many of them from the South, to Buchanan's 135, with Douglas and Cass receiving the rest. By the following morning fourteen ballots had been completed, but none of the three main candidates were able to get two-thirds of the vote. Pierce, whose support had been slowly declining as the ballots passed, directed his supporters to break for Douglas, withdrawing his name in a last-ditch effort to defeat Buchanan. Douglas, only 43 years of age, believed that he could be nominated in 1860 if he let the older Buchanan win this time, and received assurances from Buchanan's managers that this would be the case. After two more deadlocked ballots, Douglas's managers withdrew his name, leaving Buchanan as the clear winner. To soften the blow to Pierce, the convention issued a resolution of "unqualified approbation" in praise of his administration and selected his ally, Kentucky Representative <a href="/wiki/John_C._Breckinridge" title="John C. Breckinridge">John C. Breckinridge</a>, as the vice-presidential nominee.<sup id="cite_ref-1856-convention_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1856-convention-117"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce endorsed Buchanan, though the two remained distant; he hoped to resolve the Kansas situation by November to improve the Democrats' chances in the general election. He installed <a href="/wiki/John_W._Geary" title="John W. Geary">John W. Geary</a> as territorial governor, who drew the ire of pro-slavery legislators.<sup id="cite_ref-w272plus_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w272plus-118"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> Geary was able to restore order in Kansas, though the electoral damage had already been done—Republicans used "Bleeding Kansas" and "Bleeding Sumner" (the brutal <a href="/wiki/Caning_of_Charles_Sumner" title="Caning of Charles Sumner">caning of Charles Sumner</a> by South Carolina Representative <a href="/wiki/Preston_Brooks" title="Preston Brooks">Preston Brooks</a> in the Senate chamber) as election slogans.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> The Buchanan/Breckinridge ticket was elected, but the Democratic percentage of the popular vote in the North fell from 49.8 percent in 1852 to 41.4 in 1856 as Buchanan won only five of sixteen free states (Pierce had won fourteen), and in three of those, Buchanan won because of a split between the Republican candidate, former California senator <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont" title="John C. Frémont">John C. Frémont</a> and the Know Nothing, former president Fillmore.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce did not temper his rhetoric after losing the nomination. In his final message to Congress, delivered in December 1856, he vigorously attacked Republicans and abolitionists. He took the opportunity to defend his record on fiscal policy, and on achieving peaceful relations with other nations.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> In the final days of the Pierce administration, Congress passed bills to increase the pay of army officers and to build new naval vessels, also expanding the number of seamen enlisted. It also passed a tariff reduction bill he had long sought.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce and his cabinet left office on March 4, 1857, the only time in U.S. history that the original cabinet members all remained for a full four-year term.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Later life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">Post-presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Post-presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_P.A._Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Portrait of Franklin Pierce" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/George_P.A._Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-George_P.A._Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/George_P.A._Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-George_P.A._Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/George_P.A._Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-George_P.A._Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3901" data-file-height="5112" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_P.A._Healy_-_Franklin_Pierce_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pierce, seen here in 1858, remained a vocal political figure after his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-postpres_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-postpres-124"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After leaving the White House, the Pierces remained in Washington for more than two months, staying with former Secretary of State Marcy.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Buchanan altered course from the Pierce administration, replacing all of his appointees. The Pierces eventually moved to Portsmouth, New Hampshire, where Pierce had begun to speculate in property. Seeking warmer weather, he and Jane spent the next three years traveling, beginning with a stay in <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a> and followed by tours of Europe and the <a href="/wiki/Bahamas" title="Bahamas" class="mw-redirect">Bahamas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-postpres_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-postpres-124"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> In Rome, he visited Nathaniel Hawthorne; the two men spent much time together and the author found the retired president as buoyant as ever.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce never lost sight of politics during his travels, commenting regularly on the nation's growing sectarian conflict. He insisted that northern abolitionists stand down to avoid a southern secession, writing that the bloodshed of a civil war would "not be along Mason and Dixon's line merely", but "within our own borders in our own streets".<sup id="cite_ref-postpres_124-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-postpres-124"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> He also criticized New England Protestant ministers, who largely supported abolition and Republican candidates, for their "heresy and treason".<sup id="cite_ref-postpres_124-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-postpres-124"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> The rise of the Republican Party forced the Democrats to defend Pierce; during <a href="/wiki/Lincoln-Douglas_debates" title="Lincoln-Douglas debates" class="mw-redirect">his debates</a> with Republican Senate candidate <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> in 1858, Douglas called the former president "a man of integrity and honor".<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the Democratic Convention of 1860 approached, some asked Pierce to run as a compromise candidate that could unite the fractured party, but Pierce refused. As Douglas struggled to attract southern support, Pierce backed Cushing and then Breckinridge as potential alternatives, but his priority was a united Democratic Party. The split Democrats were soundly defeated for the presidency by the Republican candidate, Lincoln. In the months between Lincoln's election, and his inauguration on March 4, 1861, Pierce looked on as several southern states began plans to secede. He was asked by Justice Campbell to travel to Alabama and address that state's secession convention. Due to illness he declined, but sent a letter appealing to the people of Alabama to remain in the Union, and give the North time to repeal laws against southern interests and to find common ground.<sup id="cite_ref-secede_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-secede-128"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War">Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After efforts to prevent the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> ended with the firing on <a href="/wiki/Fort_Sumter" title="Fort Sumter">Fort Sumter</a>, Northern Democrats, including Douglas, endorsed Lincoln's plan to bring the Southern states back into the fold by force. Pierce wanted to avoid war at all costs, and wrote to Van Buren, proposing an assembly of former U.S. presidents to resolve the issue, but this suggestion was not acted on. "I will never justify, sustain or in any way or to any extent uphold this cruel, heartless, aimless, unnecessary war," Pierce wrote to his wife.<sup id="cite_ref-secede_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-secede-128"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce publicly opposed President Lincoln's order suspending the writ of <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus_in_the_United_States" title="Habeas corpus in the United States"><i>habeas corpus</i></a>, arguing that even in a time of war, the country should not abandon its protection of civil liberties. This stand won him admirers with the emerging <a href="/wiki/Copperheads_(politics)" title="Copperheads (politics)" class="mw-redirect">Northern Peace Democrats</a>, but others saw the stand as further evidence of Pierce's southern bias.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In September 1861, Pierce traveled to Michigan, visiting his former Interior Secretary, McClelland, former senator Cass, and others. A Detroit bookseller, J. A. Roys, sent a letter to Lincoln's Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/William_H._Seward" title="William H. Seward">William H. Seward</a>, accusing the former president of meeting with disloyal people, and saying he had heard there was a plot to overthrow the government and establish Pierce as president. Later that month, the pro-administration <i><a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tribune" title="Detroit Tribune">Detroit Tribune</a></i> printed an item calling Pierce "a prowling traitor spy", and intimating that he was a member of the pro-Confederate <a href="/wiki/Knights_of_the_Golden_Circle" title="Knights of the Golden Circle">Knights of the Golden Circle</a>. No such conspiracy existed, but a Pierce supporter, Guy S. Hopkins, sent to the <i>Tribune</i> a letter purporting to be from a member of the Knights of the Golden Circle, indicating that "President P." was part of a plot against the Union.<sup id="cite_ref-michigan_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michigan-130"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> Hopkins intended for the <i>Tribune</i> to make the charges public, at which point Hopkins would admit authorship, thus making the <i>Tribune</i> editors seem overly partisan and gullible. Instead, the <i>Tribune</i> editors forwarded the Hopkins letter to government officials. Seward then ordered the arrest of possible "traitors" in Michigan, which included Hopkins. Hopkins confessed authorship of the letter and admitted the hoax, but despite this, Seward wrote to Pierce demanding to know if the charges were true. Pierce denied them, and Seward hastily backtracked. Later, Republican newspapers printed the Hopkins letter in spite of his admission that it was a hoax, and Pierce decided that he needed to clear his name publicly. When Seward refused to make their correspondence public, Pierce publicized his outrage by having a Senate ally, California's <a href="/wiki/Milton_Latham" title="Milton Latham">Milton Latham</a>, read the letters between Seward and Pierce into the Congressional record, to the administration's embarrassment.<sup id="cite_ref-michigan_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michigan-130"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The institution of the draft and the arrest of outspoken anti-administration Democrat <a href="/wiki/Clement_Vallandigham" title="Clement Vallandigham">Clement Vallandigham</a> further incensed Pierce, who gave an address to New Hampshire Democrats in July 1863 vilifying Lincoln. "Who, I ask, has clothed the President with power to dictate to any one of us when we must or when we may speak, or be silent upon any subject, and especially in relation to the conduct of any public servant?", he demanded.<sup id="cite_ref-speech_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-speech-131"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce's comments were ill-received in much of the North, especially as his criticism of Lincoln's aims coincided with the twin Union victories at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gettysburg" title="Battle of Gettysburg">Gettysburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Vicksburg" title="Siege of Vicksburg">Vicksburg</a>. Pierce's reputation in the North was further damaged the following month when the Mississippi plantation of the Confederate president, Jefferson Davis, was seized by Union soldiers. Pierce's correspondence with Davis, all pre-war, revealing his deep friendship with Davis and predicting that civil war would result in insurrection in the North, was sent to the press. Pierce's words hardened abolitionist sentiment against him.<sup id="cite_ref-speech_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-speech-131"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jane Pierce died of tuberculosis in Andover, Massachusetts in December 1863; she was buried at Old North Cemetery in Concord, New Hampshire. Pierce was further grieved by his close friend Nathaniel Hawthorne's death in May 1864; he was with Hawthorne when the author died unexpectedly. Hawthorne had controversially dedicated his final book to Pierce. Some Democrats tried again to put Pierce's name up for consideration as the 1864 election unfolded, but he kept his distance; Lincoln easily won a second term. When news spread of <a href="/wiki/Lincoln%27s_assassination" title="Lincoln's assassination" class="mw-redirect">Lincoln's assassination</a> in April 1865, a mob gathered outside Pierce's home in Concord, demanding to know why he had not raised a flag to show his grief. Pierce grew angry, expressing sadness over Lincoln's death but denying any need for a public gesture. He told them that his history of military and public service proved his patriotism, which was enough to quiet the crowd.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_and_death">Final years and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Final years and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pierce's drinking worsened his health in his last years, and he grew increasingly spiritual. He had a brief relationship with an unknown woman in mid-1865. During this time, he used his influence to improve the treatment of Davis, now a prisoner at <a href="/wiki/Fortress_Monroe" title="Fortress Monroe" class="mw-redirect">Fortress Monroe</a> in Virginia. He also offered financial help to Hawthorne's son Julian, as well as to his own nephews. On the second anniversary of Jane's death, Pierce was baptized into his wife's <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(United_States)" title="Episcopal Church (United States)">Episcopal</a> faith at St. Paul's Church in Concord. He found this church to be less political than his former Congregational denomination, which had alienated Democrats with anti-slavery rhetoric. He took up the life of an "old farmer", as he called himself, buying up property and farming it himself, while entertaining visiting relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> He spent most of his time in Concord and his cottage at <a href="/wiki/Little_Boar%27s_Head_Historic_District" title="Little Boar's Head Historic District">Little Boar's Head</a> on the coast, sometimes visiting Jane's relatives in Massachusetts. Still interested in politics, he expressed support for <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction</a> policy and supported the president's acquittal in his <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_of_Andrew_Johnson" title="Impeachment of Andrew Johnson">impeachment trial</a>; he later expressed optimism for Johnson's successor, Ulysses S. Grant.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Pierce's health declined further through mid-1869, although he continued to drink. He returned to Concord that September, suffering from severe <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a> of the liver, knowing he would not recover. A caretaker was hired for him; none of his family members were present in his final days. He died at 4:35&#160;am on October 8. President Grant, who later defended Pierce's service in the Mexican War, declared a day of national mourning. Newspapers across the country carried lengthy front-page stories examining Pierce's colorful and controversial career. Pierce was interred next to his wife and two of his sons in the Minot enclosure at Concord's <a href="/wiki/Old_North_Cemetery_(Concord,_New_Hampshire)" title="Old North Cemetery (Concord, New Hampshire)">Old North Cemetery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-burial_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burial-135"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In his last <a href="/wiki/Will_(law)" title="Will (law)" class="mw-redirect">will</a>, which he signed January 22, 1868, Pierce left a large number of specific bequests such as paintings, swords, horses, and other items to friends, family, and neighbors. Much of his $72,000 estate (about $1.2 million in 2014) went to his brother Henry's family, and to Hawthorne's children and Pierce's landlady. Henry's son Frank Pierce received the largest share.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sites.2C_memorials.2C_and_honors">Sites, memorials, and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sites, memorials, and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In addition to his LL.D. from Norwich University, Pierce also received honorary doctorates from Bowdoin College (1853) and <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a> (1860).<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Two places in New Hampshire have been listed on the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> specifically because of their association with Pierce. The <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce_Homestead" title="Franklin Pierce Homestead">Franklin Pierce Homestead</a> in Hillsborough is a state park and a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a>, open to the public.<sup id="cite_ref-homestead_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-homestead-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce_House_(South_Main_Street,_Concord,_New_Hampshire)" title="Franklin Pierce House (South Main Street, Concord, New Hampshire)">Franklin Pierce House</a> in Concord, where Pierce died, was destroyed by fire in 1981, but is nevertheless listed on the register.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pierce_Manse" title="Pierce Manse">Pierce Manse</a>, his Concord home from 1842 to 1848, is open seasonally and maintained by a volunteer group, "The Pierce Brigade".<sup id="cite_ref-manse_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-manse-49"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Several institutions and places have been named after Pierce, mostly in New Hampshire. The <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce_University" title="Franklin Pierce University">Franklin Pierce University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rindge,_New_Hampshire" title="Rindge, New Hampshire">Rindge, New Hampshire</a>, was chartered in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/University_of_New_Hampshire_School_of_Law" title="University of New Hampshire School of Law">University of New Hampshire School of Law</a> was founded in 1973 as the Franklin Pierce Law Center. When the school was renamed in 2010, a Franklin Pierce Center for Intellectual Property was established.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> There is a <a href="/wiki/Mt._Pierce" title="Mt. Pierce" class="mw-redirect">Mt. Pierce</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Range" title="Presidential Range">Presidential Range</a> of New Hampshire's <a href="/wiki/White_Mountains_(New_Hampshire)" title="White Mountains (New Hampshire)">White Mountains</a>, renamed from Mt. Clinton in 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> The small town of <a href="/wiki/Pierceton,_Indiana" title="Pierceton, Indiana">Pierceton, Indiana</a>, was founded in the 1850s and honors President Pierce.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;max-width:282px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:126px;max-width:126px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin_Pierce_1938_Issue-14c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A postage stamp featuring Pierce." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Franklin_Pierce_1938_Issue-14c.jpg/124px-Franklin_Pierce_1938_Issue-14c.jpg" width="124" height="151" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Franklin_Pierce_1938_Issue-14c.jpg/186px-Franklin_Pierce_1938_Issue-14c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Franklin_Pierce_1938_Issue-14c.jpg/248px-Franklin_Pierce_1938_Issue-14c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="632" data-file-height="768" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin_Pierce_$1_Presidential_Coin_obverse_sketch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A one-dollar coin featuring Pierce." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Franklin_Pierce_%241_Presidential_Coin_obverse_sketch.jpg/150px-Franklin_Pierce_%241_Presidential_Coin_obverse_sketch.jpg" width="150" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Franklin_Pierce_%241_Presidential_Coin_obverse_sketch.jpg/225px-Franklin_Pierce_%241_Presidential_Coin_obverse_sketch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Franklin_Pierce_%241_Presidential_Coin_obverse_sketch.jpg/300px-Franklin_Pierce_%241_Presidential_Coin_obverse_sketch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1092" data-file-height="1093" /></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Pierce's image has been used on a <a href="/wiki/U.S._Presidents_on_U.S._Postage_stamps" title="U.S. Presidents on U.S. Postage stamps" class="mw-redirect">U.S. postage stamp</a> (1938) and a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_$1_Coin_Program" title="Presidential $1 Coin Program">Presidential Dollar Coin</a> (2010).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Pierce died, he mostly passed from the American consciousness, except as one of a series of presidents whose disastrous tenures led to civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-g180_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g180-143"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> Pierce's presidency is widely regarded as a failure; he is often described as one of the <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_United_States_Presidents" title="Historical rankings of United States Presidents" class="mw-redirect">worst presidents in American history</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> The public placed him third-to-last among his peers in <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a> surveys (2000 and 2009).<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> Part of his failure was in allowing a divided Congress to take the initiative, most disastrously with the Kansas–Nebraska Act. Although he did not lead that fight—Senator Douglas did—Pierce paid the cost in damage to his reputation.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> The failure of Pierce, as president, to secure sectional conciliation helped bring an end to the dominance of the Democratic Party that had started with Jackson, and led to a period of over seventy years when the Republicans mostly controlled national politics.<sup id="cite_ref-crockett_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crockett-149"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Eric_Foner" title="Eric Foner">Eric Foner</a> says, "His administration turned out to be one of the most disastrous in American history. It witnessed the collapse of the party system inherited from the Age of Jackson"<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Biographer <a href="/wiki/Roy_Nichols" title="Roy Nichols">Roy Nichols</a> argues:</p>
<dl>
<dd>As a national political leader Pierce was an accident. He was honest and tenacious of his views but, as he made up his mind with difficulty and often reversed himself before making a final decision, he gave a general impression of instability. Kind, courteous, generous, he attracted many individuals, but his attempts to satisfy all factions failed and made him many enemies. In carrying out his principles of strict construction he was most in accord with Southerners, who generally had the letter of the law on their side. He failed utterly to realize the depth and the sincerity of Northern feeling against the South and was bewildered at the general flouting of the law and the Constitution, as he described it, by the people of his own New England. At no time did he catch the popular imagination. His inability to cope with the difficult problems that arose early in his administration caused him to lose the respect of great numbers, especially in the North, and his few successes failed to restore public confidence. He was an inexperienced man, suddenly called to assume a tremendous responsibility, who honestly tried to do his best without adequate training or temperamental fitness.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Despite a reputation as an able politician and a likable man, during his presidency Pierce served only as a moderator among the increasingly bitter factions that were driving the nation towards civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> To Pierce, who saw slavery as a question of property rather than morality,<sup id="cite_ref-crockett_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crockett-149"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> the Union was sacred; because of this, he saw the actions of abolitionists, and the more moderate Free Soilers, as divisive and as a threat to the constitutionally-guaranteed rights of southerners.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> Although he criticized those who sought to limit or end slavery, he rarely rebuked southern politicians who took extreme position or opposed northern interests.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>David Potter concludes that the Ostend Manifesto and the Kansas–Nebraska Act were "the two great calamities of the Franklin Pierce administration&#160;... Both brought down an avalanche of public criticism."<sup id="cite_ref-potter_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-potter-156"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> More important, says Potter, they permanently discredited Manifest Destiny and "popular sovereignty" as political doctrines.<sup id="cite_ref-potter_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-potter-156"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> Historian Kenneth Nivison, writing in 2010, takes a more favorable view of Pierce's foreign policy, stating that his expansionism prefaced those of later presidents <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>, who served at a time when America had the military might to make her desires stick. "American foreign and commercial policy beginning in the 1890s, which eventually supplanted European colonialism by the middle of the twentieth century, owed much to the paternalism of Jacksonian Democracy cultivated in the international arena by the Presidency of Franklin Pierce."<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Historian Larry Gara, who authored a book on Pierce's presidency, wrote in the former president's entry in <i>American National Biography Online</i>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>He was president at a time that called for almost superhuman skills, yet he lacked such skills and never grew into the job to which he had been elected. His view of the Constitution and the Union was from the Jacksonian past. He never fully understood the nature or depth of Free Soil sentiment in the North. He was able to negotiate a reciprocal trade treaty with Canada, to begin the opening of Japan to western trade, to add land to the Southwest, and to sign legislation for the creation of an overseas empire [the <a href="/wiki/Guano_Islands_Act" title="Guano Islands Act">Guano Islands Act</a>]. His Cuba and Kansas policies led only to deeper sectional strife. His support for the Kansas-Nebraska Act and his determination to enforce the Fugitive Slave Act helped polarize the sections. Pierce was hard-working and his administration largely untainted by graft, yet the legacy from those four turbulent years contributed to the tragedy of secession and civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franklin_Pierce&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some local accounts suggest he was born in the Homestead. The <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> cites the log cabin as the more likely birthplace,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> while historian Peter A. Wallner asserts without reservation he was born in the log cabin.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At the time this was called the <i>Republican</i> or <i>Jeffersonian Republican Party</i>; it soon became known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a></i>. Modern writers prefer this term to distinguish it from the modern-day <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_Hampshire" title="Governor of New Hampshire">governor of New Hampshire</a> was then elected annually; see also <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_New_Hampshire" title="List of Governors of New Hampshire">List of Governors of New Hampshire</a>.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wallner writes:</span>
<dl>
<dd><span class="reference-text">It is doubtful if any former president was as reviled in later life as Franklin Pierce was, and his reputation has hardly improved in the century and a half since his death. If anything, he has been forgotten and relegated to a footnote in history books—as an amiable nonentity who had no business being president and who reached that lofty position purely by the accident of circumstance.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-legacy_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legacy-145"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></span></dd>
</dl>
</li>
</ol>
</div>

<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 22em; -webkit-column-width: 22em; column-width: 22em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Rumsch, BreAnn (2009). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=K2KAZPRuwVMC&amp;pg=PA28&amp;dq=%22franklin+pierce%22+antislavery#v=onepage&amp;q=abolition&amp;f=false"><i>Franklin Pierce:14th President of the United States</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The two-storey school building burned some years later, and Hancock Academy was founded in 1836 to fill its place (D. <li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://pierceton.us/history/">"History"</a>. Pierceton, Indiana<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 29,</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranklin+Pierce&amp;rft.btitle=History&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fpierceton.us%2Fhistory%2F&amp;rft.pub=Pierceton%2C+Indiana&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-g180-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-g180_143-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gara (1981), p. 180.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-144">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wallner (2007), pp. 377–79</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-legacy-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-legacy_145-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wallner (2004), pp. xi–xii:</span>
<dl>
<dd><span class="reference-text">History has accorded to the Pierce administration a share of the blame for policies that incited the slavery issue, hastened the collapse of the second party system, and brought on the Civil War.&#160;... It is both an inaccurate and unfair judgment. Pierce was always a nationalist attempting to find a middle ground to keep the Union together.&#160;... The alternative to attempting to steer a moderate course was the breakup of the Union, the Civil War and the deaths of more than six hundred thousand Americans. Pierce should not be blamed for attempting throughout his political career to avoid this fate.</span></dd>
</dl>
<span class="reference-text">Gara (1991), pp.&#160;180–84:</span>
<dl>
<dd><span class="reference-text">Those who play the presidential ratings game have always assigned to Franklin Pierce a below-average score.&#160;... In light of subsequent events, the Pierce administration can be seen only as a disaster for the nation. Its failure was as much a failure of the system as a failure of Pierce himself, whom Roy Franklin Nichols has skillfully portrayed as a complex and tragic figure.</span></dd>
</dl>
<span class="reference-text"><i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i>, "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.usnews.com/news/history/articles/2007/02/16/worst-presidents-franklin-pierce">Worst Presidents: Franklin Pierce</a>" (2007):</span>
<dl>
<dd><span class="reference-text">His fervor for expanding the borders helped set the stage for the Civil War.</span></dd>
</dl>
</li>
